[0:0:406,348] Natural 

[1:0:386,373] Resources 

[2:0:352,424] Natural Resources Agency programs protect and 

[2:1:352,433] restore California's diverse natural and cultural 

[2:2:352,442] resources for current and future generations, 

[3:0:352,451] including operating state parks, managing water 

[3:1:352,460] supplies, supporting forest health, encouraging 

[3:2:352,469] clean energy, and preserving fish and wildlife 

[3:3:352,478] habitats, rich farmlands and mineral resources. 

[3:4:352,487] Agency programs also protect the public through 

[3:5:352,496] suppression of wildfires and construction of 

[4:0:352,505] levees in the Central Valley. These programs not 

[4:1:352,514] only contribute to the state's unique quality of 

[4:2:352,523] life, but also are critical to sustaining a vibrant 

[4:3:352,532] economy.

[1:0:213,44] 3100 California Science Center 

[1:1:58,64] The Science Center, the Office of Exposition Park Management and the California African American Museum (CAAM), are 

[1:2:58,73] located in Exposition Park, a 160-acre tract in south Los Angeles, which is owned by the state. For budget purposes, these 

[1:3:58,82] three departments are collectively known as the California Science Center. 

[2:0:58,100] The mission of the Science Center is to stimulate curiosity and inspire science related learning. The Office of Exposition 

[2:1:58,109] Park Management provides long-term leadership in the development and implementation of park usage policy and day-to-

[2:2:58,118] day management, operation and promotion of the park for its tenants and the public. The mission of CAAM is to research, 

[2:3:58,127] collect, preserve and interpret for public enrichment, the history, art and culture of African Americans with a multi-layered 

[2:4:58,136] focus that is global, national, and local with special emphasis on California and the western United States.

[8:0:58,177] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[9:0:58,204] 2300 - EDUCATION 

[10:0:58,222] Attracting over 2 million guests annually, the Science Center-Education represents one of California's premier educational 

[10:1:58,231] and family destinations. Governed by a nine-member Board of Directors appointed by the Governor, the Science Center 

[10:2:58,240] develops and features award-winning exhibits and internationally renowned education programs. 

[11:0:58,258] Hands-on educational exhibits and programs focus on science, math, technology, and conservation which explore biological 

[11:1:58,267] processes of humans, animals, plants, the Earth's ecosystems, habitats, and geophysical processes, as well as engineering, 

[11:2:58,276] communications, and transportation, on land and in space. In 2012, the Space Shuttle Endeavour was moved to its 

[11:3:58,285] temporary home - the Samuel Oschin Pavilion - while design and construction of the Science Center's Air and Space Center 

[11:4:58,294] - its permanent home - is completed. 

[12:0:58,312] In addition, the Science Center operates a 3D IMAX theater which features science related films and documentaries. The 

[12:1:58,321] Center for Science Learning offers professional development programs to improve math and science skills of teachers and 

[12:2:58,330] other educators. The Science Center School is a K-5 science, math and technology focused neighborhood charter school. 

[12:3:58,339] As a Title I school, it serves one of the more underserved and economically challenged communities in South Los Angeles. 

[12:4:58,348] The school's instructional programs and teacher training meet California science standards. 

[13:0:58,366] The Science Center's mission values accessibility and inclusiveness and strives to inspire interest in science among those 

[13:1:58,375] traditionally underrepresented in science, math, technology and engineering. The Science Center receives significant 

[13:2:58,384] funding support for exhibit development and capital outlay projects from the California Science Center Foundation. 

[14:0:58,402] 2305 - EXPOSITION PARK MANAGEMENT 

[15:0:58,420] The Office of Exposition Park Management (OEPM) is responsible for public safety operations, parking services 

[15:1:58,429] management and overall park beautification maintenance. In addition, OEPM administers supervision and coordination of 

[15:2:58,438] park-wide events, and facilitates communication efforts between state, city, and county entities represented within the park. 

[15:3:58,447] OEPM is responsible for the negotiations of ground leases and agreements which impact the state's interest, including 

[15:4:58,456] development of park facilities and future infrastructure improvements. Exposition Park enriches the internal park community 

[15:5:58,465] and in addition provides green space and recreational areas for the surrounding neighborhood. 

[16:0:58,483] 2310 - CALIFORNIA AFRICAN AMERICAN MUSEUM 

[17:0:58,501] The California African American Museum (CAAM) is governed by a seven-member board of directors appointed by the 

[17:1:58,510] Governor. CAAM's mission is history, art and culture. The diverse exhibits are displayed on a seasonal basis in the various 

[17:2:58,519] galleries of the 44,000 square foot building. CAAM delivers its mission to the public through its history and art collections; 

[17:3:58,528] permanent, self-curated, temporary and traveling exhibits; and, innumerable programs and workshops, lectures, seminars, 

[17:4:58,537] films, educational services, standards-based curriculums, and cultural presentations. CAAM facilitates the delivery of these 

[17:5:58,546] services through dozens of outreach efforts including school bus services, docent led tours and creative workshops, through 

[17:6:58,555] Buses & Docents; CAAM Technology Arts Program; employment skill development for high school students under Young 

[17:7:58,564] Docents at CAAM: Conversations at CAAM; Young Voices at CAAM; literacy promotion through Heads are Turning Children 

[17:8:58,573] are Learning; Professional Educator's Open Houses; Films & Shorts at CAAM; off-site workshops and professionally led 

[17:9:58,582] classes in Learning Without Boundaries; and, family and cross-cultural community presentations through Target Sundays at 

[17:10:58,591] CAAM. For these and many other services CAAM is also generously supported with contributions from its 501(c)(3) partner 

[17:11:58,600] Friends, the Foundation of the California African American Museum.

[12:0:58,389] 2320 - TAHOE REGIONAL PLANNING AGENCY 

[13:0:58,407] A congressionally approved compact between California and Nevada established the Tahoe Regional Planning Agency. 

[13:1:58,416] The Agency coordinates planning and regulations that preserve and enhance the environment and resources of the Lake 

[13:2:58,425] Tahoe Basin. Funding for the Agency is shared between Nevada (one-third) and California (two-thirds). 

[13:3:58,443] 2325 - YOSEMITE FOUNDATION PROGRAM 

[14:0:58,461] This program receives revenue from the sale of personalized motor vehicle license plates by the Department of Motor 

[14:1:58,470] Vehicles. The funds are allocated by the State Controller for restoration and preservation projects in Yosemite National Park. 

[14:2:58,488] 2330 - SEA GRANT PROGRAM

[15:1:58,515] program provides funding to the University of California and University of Southern California to match federal funds for 

[15:2:58,524] selected projects under the federal Sea Grant Program.

[11:0:199,526] 3125 California Tahoe Conservancy 

[11:1:58,546] The California Tahoe Conservancy's mission is to lead California's efforts to restore and enhance the extraordinary natural 

[11:2:58,555] and recreational resources of the Lake Tahoe Basin. The Conservancy achieves its mission by developing and 

[12:0:58,564] implementing programs that acquire environmentally sensitive land, provide grants or directly fund soil erosion prevention 

[12:1:58,573] and watershed restoration, improve stream environments and wildlife habitats, and improve water quality. The Conservancy 

[12:2:58,582] also balances these activities with programs to improve public access and recreational opportunities in an environmentally 

[12:3:58,591] sound manner by acquiring lakefront property, constructing pedestrian and bike paths, and building public access facilities. 

[12:4:58,609] The Conservancy is also a key implementing agency of the Environmental Improvement Program (EIP) which is a 1997 

[12:5:58,618] agreement between California, Nevada, the Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, the federal government, local governments, 

[12:6:58,627] and various private entities to commit $908 million over 10 years to a list of acquisition and capital improvement projects 

[12:7:58,636] needed to achieve environmental goals in the Basin. The EIP is updated periodically to include more refined estimates of 

[12:8:58,645] projects, modifications in the scope of identified projects, and inclusion of new projects. The 2008-2018 EIP was finalized in 

[12:9:58,654] July 2009. 

[13:0:58,672] Because the Conservancy's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Conservancy has a capital outlay 

[13:1:58,681] program to support this need. For the specifics on the Conservancy's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview." 

[14:0:58,708] 3-YR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS  

[15:0:58,745] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[6:0:58,259] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:0:58,286] 2340 - TAHOE CONSERVANCY 

[8:0:58,304] The California Tahoe Conservancy performs day-to-day management and maintenance of the lands it has acquired, as well 

[8:1:58,313] as the completion of corrective and restorative actions needed to fully achieve the purposes of the acquisitions. It is 

[8:2:58,322] envisioned that the Conservancy ultimately will acquire up to 6,000 environmentally sensitive parcels under existing criteria. 

[8:3:58,331] To date nearly 4,900 parcels, totaling nearly 6,500 acres, have been acquired. These parcels are being managed to 

[8:4:58,340] maintain and/or restore their natural functions, provide for public safety, and provide for open spaces. 

[8:5:58,358] The Conservancy has also acquired additional parcels where more extensive site improvements or more active ultimate 

[8:6:58,367] uses are envisioned. For example, the management objectives for the public access and recreation lands are to allow for: 

[8:7:58,376] suitable and appropriate interim uses during the site improvement planning and implementation process, design and 

[8:8:58,385] construction of public access facilities, and long-term management. 

[9:0:58,403] In addition, high rates of tree mortality (25 percent-40 percent) throughout the Basin have required special emphasis on 

[9:1:58,412] forest resource management activities to reduce fuel hazards, remove hazardous conditions, start corrective actions to 

[9:2:58,421] manage forest health through a variety of means, and limit public liability. Activities include the removal of diseased, dead 

[9:3:58,430] and dying trees to promote forest health while reducing the risk of fire that would destroy property and habitat, threaten lives, 

[9:4:58,439] and diminish the water quality and other resource values of Lake Tahoe. 

[10:0:58,457] The program also provides grants to local governments to improve the Lake's water quality by controlling soil erosion caused 

[10:1:58,466] by existing development, primarily along roadways, and to treat sediment-laden runoff. These projects involve acquisitions 

[10:2:58,475] and site improvements such as the revegetation of barren slopes, and building roadside and storm drainage facilities and 

[10:3:58,484] sediment basins. 

[11:0:58,502] 2345 - ACQUISITION AND SITE IMPROVEMENT PROJECTS 

[12:0:58,520] This program acquires environmentally sensitive lands and provides public access and recreation by enhancing existing 

[12:1:58,529] facilities and establishing new public access sites along the Lake and in natural areas. The program also preserves a wide 

[12:2:58,538] variety of habitats supporting endangered, threatened or rare animal and plant species, and restores streams and 

[12:3:58,547] watersheds such as marshes, meadows and riparian areas to mitigate development damage and enhance water quality. 

[13:0:58,574] DETAILED EXPENDITURES BY PROGRAM

[1:0:164,44] 3125 California Tahoe Conservancy - Continued 

[2:0:58,89] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW 

[3:0:58,108] The Tahoe Conservancy (Conservancy) manages programs to help protect Lake Tahoe's water quality and conserve wildlife 

[3:1:58,117] habitat, watershed areas, and public access on the California side of the Lake Tahoe Basin. The Conservancy is a primary 

[3:2:58,126] implementer of the State of California's responsibilities under the Environmental Improvement Program (EIP) for the Lake 

[3:3:58,135] Tahoe Basin - a 1997 agreement between California, Nevada, the Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, the federal 

[3:4:58,144] government, local governments, and various private entities. The EIP is updated periodically to include more refined 

[3:5:58,153] estimates of projects, modifications in the scope of identified projects, and inclusion of new projects. The 2008-2018 EIP was 

[3:6:58,162] finalized in July 2009. Based on the 2008-2018 EIP, the Conservancy has identified its own EIP project list for new funding.

[3:0:186,190] 3210 Environmental Protection Program 

[3:1:58,211] The California Environmental Protection Program receives revenue from the sale of personalized motor vehicle license 

[3:2:58,220] plates by the Department of Motor Vehicles. Revenues are deposited in the California Environmental License Plate Fund. 

[3:3:58,229] Funds expended pursuant to Division 13.5 of the Public Resources Code must have one or more of the following purposes: 

[3:4:68,247] The control and abatement of air pollution, including all phases of research into the sources, dynamics and effects of 

[3:5:68,256] environmental pollutants. 

[4:0:58,244] • 

[4:1:58,262] • 

[4:2:58,271] • 

[4:3:58,280] • 

[4:4:58,289] • 

[4:5:58,307] • 

[4:6:58,325] • 

[5:0:68,265] The acquisition, preservation, restoration, or any combination thereof, of natural areas or ecological reserves. 

[5:1:68,274] Environmental education, including formal school programs and informal public education programs. 

[5:2:68,283] Protection of nongame species and threatened and endangered plants and animals. 

[6:0:68,292] Protection, enhancement, and restoration of fish and wildlife habitat and related water quality, including review of the 

[6:1:68,301] potential impact of development activities and land use changes on that habitat. 

[7:0:68,310] The purchase, on an opportunity basis, of real property consisting of sensitive natural areas for the state park system and 

[7:1:68,319] for local and regional parks. 

[8:0:68,328] Reduction or minimization of the effects of soil erosion and the discharge of sediment into the waters of the Lake Tahoe 

[8:1:68,337] region, including the restoration of disturbed wetlands and stream environment zones. 

[9:0:58,355] The appropriations and expenditures for programs and projects funded from the California Environmental License Plate 

[9:1:58,364] Fund are contained in the budgets of the various state boards, commissions and departments. 

[10:0:58,390] LEGAL CITATIONS AND AUTHORITY

[5:0:199,238] 3340 California Conservation Corps 

[5:1:58,259] The California Conservation Corps (CCC) provides young women and men the opportunity to work hard responding to fires, 

[5:2:58,268] floods and other disasters, restoring California's environment, and installing clean energy and energy conservation 

[5:3:58,277] measures at public facilities throughout the state. Through their service, the corpsmembers gain life, work, and academic 

[5:4:58,286] skills to become strong workers and citizens. 

[6:0:58,304] In addition to the CCC, there are also 13 local conservation corps located in metropolitan communities throughout the state 

[6:1:58,313] that are annually certified by the California Conservation Corps, and engage young people in conservation, recycling, 

[6:2:58,322] education, and training activities. 

[7:0:58,340] Because CCC's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the department has a capital outlay program to support 

[7:1:58,349] this need. For the specifics on the department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[14:0:58,508] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[15:0:58,536] 2360 - TRAINING AND WORK PROGRAM 

[15:1:58,554] This Program focuses on four areas: 

[16:0:58,560] • 

[17:0:68,563] Natural Resource Work: Corpsmembers protect and enhance the state's natural resources through park development, 

[17:1:68,572] trail construction, tree-planting, fire hazard reduction, watershed improvement, wildlife habitat enhancement, removal of 

[17:2:68,581] nonnative vegetation, meadow restoration, energy and water auditing and retrofitting, irrigation system installation, and 

[17:3:68,590] drought-tolerant and other landscaping. 

[18:0:58,596] • 

[19:0:68,599] Disaster Response: Corpsmembers are dispatched to fires, assisting with initial attack, mop-up and logistical support; 

[19:1:68,608] floods, filling sandbags, reinforcing levees and stabilizing hillsides; earthquakes, removing hazards and staffing disaster 

[19:2:68,617] assistance centers; oil spill cleanup; snow removal; search-and-rescues; pest infestation eradication; and homeland 

[19:3:68,626] security assistance. 

[20:0:58,632] • 

[20:1:58,650] • 

[21:0:68,635] Corpsmember Education: Corpsmembers are provided opportunities to advance their academic skills while in the CCC 

[21:1:68,644] through local adult and charter schools,and community colleges. 

[22:0:68,653] Corpsmember Development and Training: The CCC stresses the development of both a work and service ethic, which 

[22:1:68,662] includes teamwork, self-discipline, leadership, and giving back to California. Corpsmembers learn conservation principles 

[22:2:68,671] and career planning. The CCC also offers training in trail building, first aid, Hazardous Waste Operations and Emergency 

[22:3:68,680] Response, and firefighting certification, which can lead to internship opportunities with various employers in California.

[6:0:58,256] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW 

[7:0:58,274] The California Conservation Corps (CCC) operates 26 facilities statewide, including 8 residential and 18 non-residential 

[7:1:58,283] facilities in both urban and rural areas. These facilities help the CCC achieve its mission of providing employment, training, 

[7:2:58,292] and educational opportunities to young men and women, and assisting federal, state and local agencies, and nonprofit 

[7:3:58,301] entities with the conservation of California's natural resources. 

[8:0:58,319] The CCC residential facilities house 80-100 corpsmembers and operate 24 hours a day, bringing young people together 

[8:1:58,328] from across the state, increasing capacity for emergency response and conservation projects. Non-residential centers range 

[8:2:58,337] in size from 30-60 corpsmembers and allow young people with families to live at home and report to work.

[15:0:103,597] 3360 Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission 

[15:1:58,617] The Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission (Energy Commission) is responsible for ensuring a 

[15:2:58,626] reliable supply of energy to meet state needs while protecting public health, safety, and the environment. Activities include 

[15:3:58,635] the following: making energy public policy recommendations; collecting targeted energy data and ensuring data is managed 

[15:4:58,644] responsibly; developing and implementing research, development, demonstration, and deployment policies and programs 

[15:5:58,653] that promote strategic energy investments; ensuring energy facilities approved by the Energy Commission are designed, 

[15:6:58,662] constructed, operated, and decommissioned in compliance with all applicable laws, ordinances, regulations, and standards; 

[15:7:58,671] adopting progressive building and energy efficiency standards; promoting development and deployment of advanced 

[15:8:58,680] transportation technology; and supporting climate change goals through integrated resource planning, renewable energy 

[15:9:58,689] development, and system integration.

[17:0:58,631] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[18:0:58,659] 2380 - REGULATORY and PLANNING PROGRAM 

[19:0:58,677] The Regulatory and Planning program facilitates markets in providing adequate statewide energy supplies. The program 

[19:1:58,686] develops long-range projections for future energy supplies and demand, and maintains current information on statewide 

[19:2:58,695] electricity generation, transmission, natural gas, and fuels markets. Additionally, this program certifies power plant sites, 

[19:3:58,704] designates transmission line corridors, and monitors new energy facilities and fuel infrastructure for compliance and supply 

[19:4:58,713] and distribution adequacy consistent with state energy policies. The Energy Assessments program develops long-range 

[19:5:58,722] projections for future energy supplies and demand, maintains current information on statewide electrical generation,

[2:0:58,73] consumption and peak demand, natural gas, and distributed generation. The program develops state energy policy based

[2:2:58,91] and monitors energy markets for competitive prices and potential market abuses. 

[3:0:58,109] 2380010 - Power Plant Site Certification and Transmission Line Corridor Designation Program 

[4:0:58,127] The Power Plant Site Certification and Transmission Line Corridor Designation program is responsible for the following: 

[4:1:58,136] reviewing power plant siting applications 50 megawatts and larger; ensuring power plant developers comply with conditions 

[4:2:58,145] of certification and all applicable laws and regulations when constructing and operating power plants; reviewing petitions to 

[4:3:58,154] amend siting decisions; reviewing applications for designation as transmission line corridors; evaluating constraints and 

[4:4:58,163] opportunities for energy resource development; and analyzing reliability, efficiency, and environmental performance issues 

[4:5:58,172] related to power plants and the state electricity transmission grid. 

[5:0:58,190] 2380019 - Electricity Analysis 

[6:0:58,208] The Electricity Analysis program is responsible for evaluating current and future electricity supply market trends and 

[6:1:58,217] infrastructure needs; estimating costs of various generation types; assessing the adequacy of electricity supplies; and 

[6:2:58,226] analyzing regulations and environmental issues related to electricity. Additionally, this program addresses the distribution 

[6:3:58,235] system and its relationship to distributed generation, including planning and interconnection barriers and provides 

[6:4:58,244] information and recommendations to state agencies, electricity market participants, and the public. The natural gas program 

[6:5:58,253] component is responsible for evaluating current and future natural gas market trends and infrastructure needs, assessing 

[6:6:58,262] the adequacy of natural gas supplies, monitoring the interaction of natural gas and electricity to maintain system reliability, 

[6:7:58,271] analyzing regulations and environmental issues related to natural gas, and leading interagency work groups to monitor 

[6:8:58,280] short-term natural gas market trends. The program also collects, monitors, and analyzes data on transportation fuel supply 

[6:9:58,289] and production, fuel infrastructure issues, and responds to energy and fuel shortages and emergencies. 

[7:0:58,307] 2385 - ENERGY RESOURCES CONSERVATION PROGRAM 

[8:0:58,325] The Energy Resources Conservation program objectives include reducing overall energy use and decreasing peak 

[8:1:58,334] electricity demand by identifying energy efficiency opportunities and developing and implementing programs and standards 

[8:2:58,343] for efficiently using energy in all sectors. 

[9:0:58,361] 2385010 - Building and Appliances Program 

[10:0:58,379] The Building and Appliances program objectives include reducing consumer and business energy use and peak demands 

[10:1:58,388] by developing and implementing strategies and minimum efficiency standards for new and existing residential and 

[10:2:58,397] nonresidential buildings, appliances, and equipment. The Building and Appliances program provides technical assistance on 

[10:3:58,406] building standards and enforcement support to the building industry and building departments. Further, the program 

[10:4:58,415] maintains a database of energy efficiency appliances and equipment, and enforces compliance with the appliance efficiency 

[10:5:58,424] standards. 

[11:0:58,442] 2385019 - Energy Projects Evaluation and Assistance Program 

[12:0:58,460] This program is responsible for providing technical assistance, grants, and loans to improve energy efficiency and reduce 

[12:1:58,469] operating costs of schools, colleges, local jurisdictions, hospitals, and public care facilities. 

[13:0:58,487] 2385028 - Demand Analysis 

[14:0:58,505] The Demand Analysis program is responsible for collecting and analyzing electricity and natural gas consumption data used 

[14:1:58,514] to prepare energy demand forecasts, forecasting peak and total energy consumption by sector, and estimating the amount 

[14:2:58,523] of energy conserved by existing and proposed program activities. The program also performs transportation energy demand 

[14:3:58,532] forecasting, conducts consumer vehicle choice surveys, and assesses the impacts of transportation electrification on 

[14:4:58,541] California electricity use. 

[15:0:58,559] 2390 - DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM 

[16:0:58,577] The Development Program focuses on transportation and alternatives to conventional fossil fuels. The mission is to support 

[16:1:58,586] provision of adequate and reliable transportation energy to the California transportation sector while balancing economic, 

[16:2:58,595] public health, safety, and environmental consequences. The program also conducts research, development, demonstration, 

[16:3:58,604] commercialization, and deployment activities on energy efficiency, renewable and advanced energy technologies, and 

[16:4:58,613] alternative and renewable fuel and advanced vehicle technologies so that future energy supplies are cost effective, secure, 

[16:5:58,622] and reliable; enhance environmental quality; and promote state and local economic development. The program provides 

[16:6:58,631] technical assistance, financial assistance, direct technology research and demonstration, technology forecasting, technology 

[16:7:58,640] analysis and evaluation, and information transfer. 

[17:0:58,658] 2390010 - Transportation Technology and Fuels 

[18:0:58,676] The Transportation Technology and Fuels program provides financial incentives to develop and deploy innovative 

[18:1:58,685] technologies and strategies that transform California's fuel and vehicle types to help attain the state's climate change 

[18:2:58,694] policies. The program goal is the development and deployment of low-carbon alternative fuels and advanced vehicle 

[18:3:58,703] technologies in the marketplace, without adopting any one preferred fuel or technology. The program provides analyses and 

[18:4:58,712] recommendations to guide state energy policy and legislation to achieve climate change and air quality objectives, reduce 

[18:5:58,721] petroleum consumption, and promote economic development. The program also provides incentives to workforce

[7:0:162,292] 3370 Renewable Resources Investment Program 

[7:1:58,312] The Renewable Resources Investment Program receives 30 percent of the royalties deposited in the Geothermal Resources 

[7:2:58,321] Development Account. The state receives these monies from the federal government for geothermal leases. Funds from the 

[7:3:58,330] Bosco-Keene Renewable Resources Investment Fund may be expended only for the following: fish habitat improvements; 

[7:4:58,339] forest resource improvements; urban forestry projects; agricultural soil drainage and soil erosion programs; agricultural, 

[7:5:58,348] industrial and urban water conservation; wildland fire protection; and coastal resource enhancement projects. Specific 

[7:6:58,357] project and program expenditures for the Renewable Resources Investment Program are included in the budgets of various 

[7:7:58,366] departments. 

[8:0:58,384] Beginning with the 2017-18 Governor's Budget, the Fund Condition Statement for the Bosco-Keene Renewable Resources 

[8:1:58,393] Development Account is located in the Department of Conservation budget (3480). The Fund Condition Statement for the 

[8:2:58,402] Geothermal Resources Development Account remains in the Energy Resources Conservation and Development 

[8:3:58,411] Commission budget (3360). 

[9:0:58,438] LEGAL CITATIONS AND AUTHORITY 

[9:1:58,456] DEPARTMENT AUTHORITY 

[10:0:58,474] Public Resources Code, Sections 3825 and 34000. 

[11:0:187,523] 3460 Colorado River Board of California 

[11:1:58,543] The Colorado River Board protects California's rights and interests in the water and power resources of the Colorado River 

[11:2:58,552] system. The Board works with: Colorado River Basin states (Arizona, California, Colorado, Nevada, New Mexico, Utah, and 

[11:3:58,561] Wyoming), federal agencies, other state agencies, six local agencies (Palo Verde Irrigation District, Imperial Irrigation 

[11:4:58,570] District, Coachella Valley Water District, Metropolitan Water District of Southern California, San Diego County Water 

[11:5:58,579] Authority, Los Angeles Department of Water and Power), Congress, the courts, and Mexico. Its activities include analyses of 

[11:6:58,588] engineering, legal and economic matters concerning the Colorado River resources of the seven basin states and the 1944 

[11:7:58,597] United States-Mexico Water Treaty obligation to deliver Colorado River water to Mexico.

[8:1:58,290] Totals, Budget Adjustments   $152   $39,606   -   $133   $24,114   13.0
[9:0:58,322] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[10:0:58,350] 2420 - GEOLOGIC HAZARDS AND MINERAL RESOURCES CONSERVATION 

[11:0:58,368] This program evaluates, assesses and maps the state's geologic and seismologic hazards, such as earthquakes, landslides, 

[11:1:58,377] tsunami and volcanic eruption threats, and hazardous minerals exposures, in order to protect the public health and safety 

[11:2:58,386] and the natural environment; analyzes the state's mineral assets and maps its mineral resources. Information is used by 

[11:3:58,395] federal, state, and local government agencies, industries and individual businesses, and the public to make informed 

[11:4:58,404] decisions about land use, seismic safety, and mineral development. 

[11:5:58,422] 2425 - OIL, GAS, AND GEOTHERMAL RESOURCES 

[12:0:58,440] This program regulates the drilling, operation, and abandonment of oil, natural gas, and geothermal wells to protect the 

[12:1:58,449] environment, prevent pollution, and ensure public safety. The state is fully reimbursed for program expenditures by annual 

[12:2:58,458] assessments and fees on the respective industries. Approximately 450 companies operate over 88,000 wells in California for 

[12:3:58,467] the production of oil, natural gas, and geothermal resources. 

[12:4:58,485] 2430 - LAND RESOURCE PROTECTION 

[13:0:58,503] This program protects agricultural farmland and open space through various financial incentives. Under the Land 

[13:1:58,512] Conservation Act, landowners who agree to keep their property undeveloped for at least ten years receive lower property tax 

[13:2:58,521] assessments. The California Farmland Conservancy Program provides grants to local governments and nonprofit land trusts 

[13:3:58,530] for the acquisition of agricultural conservation easements that permanently remove development rights, and therefore 

[13:4:58,539] development pressure, from agricultural lands. The Farmland Mapping and Monitoring Program within the Land Resource 

[13:5:58,548] Protection Program develops maps, statistics, and reports relating to farmland conversion, farmland inventory, and land 

[13:6:58,557] protection to assist in local land use decisions. 

[13:7:58,575] 2435 - MINE RECLAMATION 

[14:0:58,593] This program regulates active surface mining operations and monitors local lead agencies to ensure compliance with the 

[14:1:58,602] Surface Mining and Reclamation Act of 1975. It assists cities, counties, state agencies, and mine operators in their efforts to 

[14:2:58,611] reclaim mined lands to beneficial uses. This program also compiles an inventory of the state's estimated 47,000 abandoned 

[14:3:58,620] mines and remediates abandoned mine hazards to protect public safety. 

[14:4:58,638] 2440 - STATE MINING AND GEOLOGY BOARD 

[15:0:58,656] The Board serves as a regulatory and policy body for the state's geology, geologic and seismologic hazards, conservation of 

[15:1:58,665] mineral resources, and reclamation of mined lands. The Department's California Geological Survey and the Office of Mine 

[15:2:58,674] Reclamation provide the engineering, technical expertise, and support functions for the reports, plans, and maps which the 

[15:3:58,683] Board approves. The Board also serves as an appeals body for mining operations that have been issued notices of 

[15:4:58,692] violations or orders to comply, and disputes with local lead agencies. 

[16:0:58,745] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[9:0:163,482] 3540 Department of Forestry and Fire Protection 

[9:1:58,503] The California Department of Forestry and Fire Protection (CAL FIRE) serves and safeguards the people and protects the 

[9:2:58,512] property and resources of California. 

[10:0:58,530] CAL FIRE provides all hazard emergency - fire, medical, rescue and disaster - response to the public and provides 

[10:1:58,539] leadership in the protection of life, property and natural resources. 

[11:0:58,557] CAL FIRE safeguards the public through: engineering; research, development and adoption of regulations; fire and life 

[11:1:58,566] safety programs; fire prevention, law enforcement, and public information & education; resource protection; and emergency 

[11:2:58,575] response. 

[12:0:58,593] CAL FIRE limits the damage caused by fires, disasters, environmental degradation, and related emergencies by employing 

[12:1:58,602] diverse yet complementary efforts including: training California's fire service professionals; public education and prevention 

[12:2:58,611] awareness; responsible stewardship of our natural resources; and natural resource and emergency management. 

[12:3:58,629] CAL FIRE's highly trained professionals and leaders cultivate mutually beneficial and cost-effective relationships with all 

[12:4:58,638] levels of government and the private sector to enhance the efficiency and effectiveness of public safety programs and 

[12:5:58,647] mitigate emergencies in the urban, rural, and wild land areas of California. 

[13:0:58,665] CAL FIRE promotes a culture of innovation, accountability, integrity, diversity, planning, workplace safety, and teamwork to 

[13:1:58,674] foster an efficient and effective organizational environment. 

[14:0:58,692] Because CAL FIRE's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the department has a capital outlay program to 

[14:1:58,701] support this need. For the specifics on the department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview." 

[15:0:58,745] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[5:0:58,251] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES 

[6:0:58,267] • 

[7:0:68,270] Climate Adaptation Extended Fire Season - The Budget includes $42 million General Fund and $309,000 various special 

[7:1:68,279] funds and reimbursements to expand the state's firefighting capabilities and extend the fire season. Specifically, this 

[7:2:68,288] proposal: (1) adds 42 year-round engines, (2) staffs CAL FIRE engines and helitack bases one month earlier in spring, 

[7:3:68,297] and (3) extends peak staffing in fall by approximately two weeks. These additional resources will also allow CAL FIRE to 

[7:4:68,306] complete a greater number of fuel reduction projects during the off-peak season, helping to reduce the fire risk from 

[7:5:68,315] climate change, drought and tree mortality. 

[8:0:58,330] • 

[9:0:68,333] Drought Legacy and Tree Mortality Response - The Budget includes $38.7 million General Fund and $3 million State 

[9:1:68,342] Responsibility Area Fire Prevention Fund for expanded fire protection in the 2017 fire season in recognition of continuing 

[9:2:68,351] tree mortality. These funds will support the continuation of firefighter surge capacity, California Conservation Corps fire 

[9:3:68,360] crews, and exclusive use of one large air tanker. In addition, $10 million State Responsibility Area Fire Prevention Fund is 

[9:4:68,369] available for grants to support local fire prevention activities.

[13:0:58,469] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[14:0:58,497] 2460 - STATE FIRE MARSHAL 

[15:0:58,506] The Office of the State Fire Marshal is moving to Program 2461. See description below.  

[15:1:58,524] 2461 - STATE FIRE MARSHAL 

[16:0:58,533] The Office of the State Fire Marshal is responsible for the statewide protection of life and property through the development 

[16:1:58,542] and application of fire protection engineering, education and enforcement. 

[16:2:58,560] 2461010 - Office of the State Fire Marshal (OSFM) includes the following activities: 

[17:0:58,566] • 

[18:0:68,569] Code Development and Analysis: Objectives include the development and adoption of codes relating to fire/life safety 

[18:1:68,578] used statewide by local fire and building authorities. The Code Development and Analysis Division fosters, promotes and 

[18:2:68,587] develops ways and means of protecting life and property against fire in many ways, including the adoption and 

[18:3:68,596] implementation of regulations for statewide application. This division prepares the OSFM fire and life safety regulations 

[18:4:68,605] and building standards for review and adoption into the California Code of Regulations, Titles 19 and 24. The division 

[18:5:68,614] assists with the application of state laws, regulations, and code enforcement relating by a city, county, fire departments or 

[18:6:68,623] fire districts, and building departments. This division also reviews all of California's regulations relating to fire and life 

[18:7:68,632] safety for relevancy, necessity, conflict, duplication and/or overlap. 

[19:0:58,638] • 

[20:0:68,641] Fire and Life Safety: This program is the main entity within the department for the application of laws and regulations 

[20:1:68,650] related to fire prevention and life safety. This is achieved through code compliance inspections of new and existing 

[20:2:68,659] buildings and plan review and construction inspections of state-owned and state-occupied projects. The OSFM is 

[20:3:68,668] responsible for fire & life safety in over 45,000 state-owned and state-occupied facilities, which include: state prisons, 

[20:4:68,677] conservation camps, community correctional facilities, state mental hospitals, state developmental centers, California 

[20:5:68,686] State University and University of California campuses, and California Agricultural Districts. 

[20:6:68,695] Fire Engineering and Arson and Bomb: The Fire Engineering Division administers licensing programs and provides 

[20:7:68,704] services for product evaluation, certifications and listings. This division also oversees the Arson and Bomb Unit, which has 

[20:8:68,713] the responsibility to dispose seized illegal fireworks and conduct fire/explosion investigations and licensing enforcement. 

[20:9:68,722] The division is responsible for 11 statewide programs, which include the Building Materials Listing; Portable Fire 

[21:0:58,692] • 

[22:0:58,745] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:128,44] 3540 Department of Forestry and Fire Protection - Continued 

[2:0:68,73] Extinguishers; Flame Retardant Chemicals and Fabrics; Vapor Recovery; Automatic Fire Extinguishing Systems; Fire 

[2:1:68,82] Safe Cigarettes; Licensing Enforcement and Inspection; Lab Accreditation; Fireworks; and Motion Picture and 

[2:2:68,91] Entertainment, Arson and Bomb Investigation and Fireworks Disposal. This division's primary functions are licensing, 

[2:3:68,100] product approval, fire/explosion investigations and licensing enforcement. 

[3:0:58,107] • 

[4:0:68,109] Pipeline Safety: This program regulates approximately 4,800 miles of intrastate hazardous liquid pipelines that transport 

[4:1:68,118] crude oil and refined products (e.g., gasoline, diesel, jet fuel, etc.) between offshore platforms, production fields, refineries 

[4:2:68,127] and marine terminals. Regulated pipelines do not include those within production fields, marine terminals or refineries. 

[4:3:68,136] State Fire Training: This program administers the California Fire Service Training and Education System and the Fire 

[4:4:68,145] Service Training and Education Program for federal, state and local firefighters. The program oversees a California Fire 

[4:5:68,154] Academy System for over 50 training academies that represent a partnership with the fire departments, community 

[4:6:68,163] colleges and the OSFM. State Fire Training administers a professional certification system for firefighters and chief 

[4:7:68,172] officers and receives program guidance from the State Board of Fire Services. 

[5:0:58,134] • 

[6:0:58,190] 2461019 - California Underground Facilities Safe Excavation Board: 

[7:0:58,199] The Board, created by the passage of Chapter 809, Statutes of 2016 (SB 661), works on issues related to the State's "Call 

[7:1:58,208] Before You Dig" law, which requires excavators, except in the case of an emergency, to contact a regional notification 

[7:2:58,217] center, as specified. 

[8:0:58,235] 2465 - FIRE PROTECTION 

[9:0:58,253] CAL FIRE provides fire protection to keep damages to life, property and natural resources within social, political and 

[9:1:58,262] economic constraints. The objective is to quickly and aggressively attack all fires in areas where CAL FIRE has assumed 

[9:2:58,271] primary direct protection responsibility by virtue of law, contract or agreement until the fire is under control. The level of initial 

[9:3:58,280] attack and follow-up action is relative to values threatened and control difficulty with the goal to control all unwanted fires 

[9:4:58,289] within the first burning period. 

[10:0:58,307] 2465010 - Fire Prevention: 

[11:0:58,316] This program is responsible for implementation of the Strategic Fire Plan, fire engineering, law enforcement, fire suppression 

[11:1:58,325] cost recovery and fire prevention education. This program focuses on the most effective methods, materials and procedures 

[11:2:58,334] to mitigate hazard and to enforce pertinent laws that deter and mitigate fires. More specifically, efforts focus on what needs 

[11:3:58,343] to be done before a wildland fire starts in order to reduce the costs of fire fighting, property loss, injury to the public and fire 

[11:4:58,352] fighters and damage to the environment. 

[12:0:58,370] 2465019 - Fire Control: 

[13:0:58,379] The objective of this program is to detect, respond and suppress wildland fires in or threatening the State Responsibility 

[13:1:58,388] Area. The heart of the effort is an aggressive initial attack strategy. CAL FIRE's goal is to contain 95 percent of all wildfires to 

[13:2:58,397] 10 acres or less. This is achieved through detection, ground attack, air attack and mutual aid using fire engines, fire crews, 

[13:3:58,406] bulldozers, helicopters and fixed wing aircraft. 

[14:0:58,424] 2465028 - Cooperative Fire Protection: 

[15:0:58,433] CAL FIRE provides a full range of fire protection services to local, county, state and federal agencies throughout California 

[15:1:58,442] through the administration of over 100 cooperative government agreements. Additionally, there are six long standing 

[15:2:58,451] agreements wherein counties provide wildland fire protection on behalf of the Department. 

[16:0:58,469] 2465037 - Conservation Camps: 

[17:0:58,478] CAL FIRE, in cooperation with the Department of Corrections and Rehabilitation, Divisions of Adult Operations and Juvenile 

[17:1:58,487] Justice, operates 39 conservation camps throughout the state that house 196 fire crews. These crews provide one of the 

[17:2:58,496] primary labor forces for firefighting, emergency response, and conservation related work projects. 

[18:0:58,514] 2465046 - Emergency Fire Suppression: 

[19:0:58,523] CAL FIRE incurs additional emergency fire suppression expenditures when budgeted initial attack forces are unable to cope 

[19:1:58,532] with a wildland fire within the initial attack period. Portions of these costs may be reimbursed in the fiscal year the expense 

[19:2:58,541] was incurred or in a future fiscal year via Fire Management Assistance Grants provided by the Federal Emergency 

[19:3:58,550] Management Agency (FEMA) or through cost shares provided by other government entities. In addition, CAL FIRE may 

[19:4:58,559] recover portions of this cost through civil cost recovery actions authorized by state statutes. 

[20:0:58,577] 2470 - RESOURCE MANAGEMENT 

[21:0:58,595] California's state and private forest, range, watershed lands and urban forests provide multiple human, climate and 

[21:1:58,604] environmental benefits. The objective of this program is to maintain and enhance those benefits and to minimize damage to 

[21:2:58,613] these resources from natural catastrophes and human development. Objectives are met by regulation of timber harvesting, 

[21:3:58,622] coordination of climate related activities for the forest sector, technical assistance to non-industrial landowners, operation of 

[21:4:58,631] state demonstration forests, operation of forest nurseries, vegetation management projects and administration of federal 

[21:5:58,640] forestry assistance programs. 

[22:0:58,658] 2470010 - Resources Protection and Improvement: 

[23:0:58,667] The objective of this program is to improve forest lands and urban forests. Activities include the detection, evaluation and 

[23:1:58,676] control of forest pests; growing and selling tree seedlings for reforestation, carbon sequestration, and soil erosion control; 

[23:2:58,685] maintenance of a native conifer seed bank; advice and assistance to non-industrial forest landowners on management of 

[23:3:58,694] forests and improved harvesting and conservation practices; research on and demonstration of sustainable forestry in state 

[23:4:58,703] forests; implementation of the California Forest Improvement Act of 1978; technical assistance and grants to foster the 

[23:5:58,712] environmental, social, and economic benefits of urban forests consistent with the California Urban Forestry Act of 1978; and 

[23:6:58,721] purchase and monitoring of working forest conservation easements per the California Forest Legacy Act of 2008. In the 

[24:0:58,745] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:2:58,91] yields and air quality, reduce carbon emissions from wildland fires, increase firefighter safety, and maintain desirable

[3:0:58,163] 2470019 - Forest Practice Regulations: 

[4:0:58,172] The objective of this program is to maximize sustained production of high-quality timber products while protecting soil, water, 

[4:1:58,181] wildlife, recreation and other values associated with forest land. Forest Practice activities include the review and permitting 

[4:2:58,190] of commercial timber harvesting operations on nonfederal timberlands, enforcing state forest practice laws and regulations, 

[4:3:58,199] and monitoring compliance and the effects of timber operations on forest productivity, water quality, watersheds, soils, 

[4:4:58,208] botanical resources, wildlife, fisheries, greenhouse gases and other environmentally sensitive resources. The Program is 

[4:5:58,217] also responsible for issuing licenses to timber operators (LTOs), providing LTO training and education, and assisting the 

[4:6:58,226] Board of Equalization-Timber Tax Division in their administration of taxes on timber and timberland. Forest Practice 

[4:7:58,235] Program staff also assist the Board of Forestry and Fire Protection, and facilitate and participate in monitoring the 

[4:8:58,244] effectiveness of the Board's forest practice regulations. 

[5:0:58,262] 2470028 - Forest Resource Inventory and Assessment: 

[6:0:58,271] The objective of this program is to perform a periodic assessment of California's forest and rangeland resources. Activities 

[6:1:58,280] include maintaining and distributing mission-critical datasets for fire planning, emergency incident support and meeting 

[6:2:58,289] certain regulatory requirements. In addition, the program provides extensive technical and public information for statewide 

[6:3:58,298] fire threat, fire hazard, watersheds, socio-economic conditions, environmental indicators and urban forestry. 

[7:0:58,316] 2470037 - Professional Foresters Registration Program: 

[8:0:58,325] The Office of Professional Foresters Registration is a statutorily authorized program within the State Board of Forestry and 

[8:1:58,334] Fire Protection. The program is responsible for the licensing and regulation of all persons who practice forestry or rangeland 

[8:2:58,343] management on non-federal, state and private lands in California pursuant to the Professional Foresters Law of 1972. 

[9:0:58,361] 2475 - BOARD OF FORESTRY AND FIRE PROTECTION 

[10:0:58,379] The California State Board of Forestry and Fire Protection (Board) is a Governor-appointed body within CAL FIRE. The 

[10:1:58,388] Board is responsible for developing the general forest policy of the state, for determining the guidance policies of CAL FIRE, 

[10:2:58,397] and for representing the state's interest in Federal land in California. Together, the Board and CAL FIRE work to carry out 

[10:3:58,406] the California Legislature's mandate to protect and enhance the State's unique forest and wildland resources. The Board's 

[10:4:58,415] statutory responsibilities are:

[16:0:58,532] 9900100 - ADMINISTRATION

[8:0:58,344] The Department of Forestry and Fire Protection operates more than 530 facilities statewide, including 234 forest fire 

[8:1:58,353] stations, 112 telecommunications facilities, 39 conservation camps, 21 unit headquarters, 16 administrative headquarters,

[8:3:58,371] facilities support fire protection, the Office of the State Fire Marshal, and resource management efforts for more than 31 

[8:4:58,380] million acres of state and privately-owned wildlands throughout the state.

[0:0:58,15] 2017-18 STATE BUDGET — RES 90   NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:214,44] 3560 State Lands Commission 

[1:1:58,64] The California State Lands Commission serves the people of California by providing stewardship of the lands, waterways, 

[1:2:58,73] and resources entrusted to its care through preservation, restoration, enhancement, responsible economic development, 

[1:3:58,82] and the promotion of public access. Diligent execution of the Commission's responsibilities since its inception in 1938 has 

[1:4:58,91] resulted in the generation of over $11 billion in revenues while protecting and enhancing the public's ability to enjoy those 

[1:5:58,100] lands and resources. 

[2:0:58,118] Consisting of the Lieutenant Governor, the State Controller, and the Governor's Director of Finance, the California State 

[2:1:58,127] Lands Commission serves as a trustee for the people of the state, managing California's sovereign public trust lands and 

[2:2:58,136] resources, which the state received upon admission into the Union in 1850. It also manages other lands subsequently 

[2:3:58,145] conveyed to the state by the federal government (commonly known as "school" lands) and oversees the management of 

[2:4:58,154] public trust lands legislatively granted in trust to over 70 local jurisdictions. These grants encourage development and use of 

[2:5:58,163] the state's tidelands consistent with the public trust doctrine, and typically require grantees to reinvest revenues produced 

[2:6:58,172] from the granted lands back into the trust. 

[3:0:58,190] Public trust or "sovereign" lands include the beds of all natural and navigable waterways, including non-tidal rivers, streams 

[3:1:58,199] and lakes, and tide and submerged lands within tidal rivers, sloughs, bays and the Pacific Ocean extending from the mean 

[3:2:58,208] high tide line seaward to the three-mile offshore limit and totaling over four and one-half million acres. In addition to 

[3:3:58,217] sovereign lands, the Commission manages "school" lands, which were granted to California by the federal government 

[3:4:58,226] under the Act of March 3, 1853 (10 Stat. 244) for the purpose of supporting public education in California. School lands 

[3:5:58,235] include the 16th and 36th sections of each township (with the exception of lands already reserved for public use or 

[3:6:58,244] previously taken by private claims) and lands known to be mineral in character. Of the five and one-half million acres of 

[3:7:58,253] school lands originally granted to the state, only about 460,370 acres remain in state ownership and these are mostly 

[3:8:58,262] concentrated in the California desert. The Commission also retains a reserved mineral interest in approximately 790,000 

[3:9:58,271] acres of sold school lands.

[13:0:58,519] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[14:0:58,546] 2560 - MINERAL RESOURCES MANAGEMENT 

[15:0:58,564] The State Lands Commission oversees efficient and safe development of mineral resources that are located on state lands. 

[15:1:58,573] The State also has a beneficial interest in the Long Beach tidelands oils fields, and as such, the Commission participates in 

[15:2:58,582] the safety and fiscal oversight of these fields. The program objectives are to: manage the responsible extraction of oil, gas, 

[15:3:58,591] geothermal resources and other minerals; collect revenues generated consistent with the best interests of the state, and 

[15:4:58,600] ensure that public safety and environmental protection are maintained at the highest possible standards in the development 

[15:5:58,609] of these resources through engineering review, marine facility inspections, and safety audits. The Commission also adopts 

[15:6:58,618] regulations for the prevention of oil spills, the safe operation of offshore facilities and program and financial audits to ensure 

[15:7:58,627] the state receives fair value for the development of its resources. 

[15:8:58,645] 2565 - LAND MANAGEMENT 

[16:0:58,663] The State Lands Commission manages all ungranted state sovereign lands consistent with the common law public trust 

[16:1:58,672] doctrine and prudent land use and management practices. The Commission issues leases and permits for the use and 

[16:2:58,681] occupation of public trust lands under its jurisdiction based upon environmental, economic, health, safety and public benefit 

[16:3:58,690] considerations. The program also manages all state school lands to ensure the greatest economic return to the State 

[16:4:58,699] Teachers' Retirement System. The Commission also oversees the administration of public trust lands legislatively granted to 

[16:5:58,708] local jurisdictions to manage on behalf of the State, including the major ports in the State. 

[16:6:58,726] 2570 - MARINE ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION PROGRAM 

[17:0:58,745] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:58,15] 2017-18 STATE BUDGET — RES 92   NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:179,44] 3560 State Lands Commission - Continued 

[2:0:58,73] The State Lands Commission adopts rules, regulations, and guidelines for marine oil terminals within California, providing 

[2:1:58,82] the best achievable protection of the public health and safety and the environment. The Commission's Marine Environmental 

[2:2:58,91] Protection Program monitors the transfer of oil at marine terminals, inspects all marine facilities and reviews, and approves 

[2:3:58,100] all marine oil terminal operations manuals and training and certification programs . The Program also administers Chapter 

[2:4:58,109] 31F of the California Building Code to ensure that marine oil terminals are fit for purpose. In addition, the Marine 

[2:5:58,118] Environmental Protection Program implements the state's Marine Invasive Species Program to prevent the introduction of 

[2:6:58,127] non-indigenous species into California waters through vessel mediated vectors. The program funds and conducts scientific 

[2:7:58,136] research, develops rules and regulations to implement the Marine Invasive Species Act, and physically inspects oceangoing 

[2:8:58,145] vessels to assess compliance with vessel-reported nonindigenous species management actions. 

[2:9:58,163] 9900100 - EXECUTIVE AND ADMINISTRATION 

[3:0:58,181] The Executive and Administration program provides management, policy direction and administrative support to the line 

[3:1:58,190] programs of the Commission.

[4:0:195,192] 3600 Department of Fish and Wildlife 

[4:1:58,212] The mission of the Department of Fish and Wildlife is to manage California's diverse fish, wildlife, and plant resources, and 

[4:2:58,221] the habitats upon which they depend, for their ecological values and for their use and enjoyment by the public. 

[4:3:58,239] This includes habitat protection and maintenance in a sufficient amount and quality to ensure the survival of all species and 

[4:4:58,248] natural communities. The Department is also responsible for the diversified use of fish and wildlife including recreational, 

[4:5:58,257] commercial, scientific, and educational uses. 

[5:0:58,275] Because the Department of Fish and Wildlife programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a 

[5:1:58,284] capital outlay program to support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure 

[5:2:58,293] Overview."

[6:0:58,260] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES 

[7:0:68,276] • 

[8:0:78,279] Pursuing Collaborative Solutions to Water Supply Reliability and Watershed Health - $1.1 million General Fund and five 

[8:1:78,288] positions for the Department of Fish and Wildlife to provide legal, scientific, and other expertise. Voluntary efforts are 

[8:2:78,297] needed to integrate watershed restoration projects with updated river flow regimes to help salmon and other fisheries 

[8:3:78,306] thrive. Agreements would describe additional water flows and habitat restoration and other measures in the major rivers 

[8:4:78,315] that flow to the Delta. 

[9:0:68,330] • 

[10:0:78,333] Concluding Drought Response - $2.6 million ongoing General Fund to sustain resilient systems for protection of fish 

[10:1:78,342] and wildlife affected by future drought and climate change. During the recent drought, significant investments have 

[10:2:78,351] been made to ensure that the state is better prepared to mitigate the harmful effects of future droughts on fish and 

[10:3:78,360] wildlife resources. These resiliency measures include installation of filtration equipment at fish hatcheries, improved 

[10:4:78,369] water use efficiency at wildlife areas, and a network of fish and wildlife monitoring systems throughout the state.

[5:0:58,259] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:58,286] 2590 - BIODIVERSITY CONSERVATION PROGRAM 

[7:0:58,304] This program encourages the preservation, conservation, maintenance, and restoration of wildlife resources, including the 

[7:1:58,313] Ecosystem Restoration Program, under the jurisdiction and influence of the state. Activities involve the conservation, 

[7:2:58,322] protection and management of fish, wildlife, native plants, and habitat to ensure maintenance of biologically sustainable 

[7:3:58,331] populations of those species. 

[8:0:58,349] 2595 - HUNTING, FISHING, AND PUBLIC USE PROGRAM 

[9:0:58,367] This program facilitates diverse and sustainable hunting, fishing (recreational and commercial), trapping, and other public 

[9:1:58,376] uses and associated economic benefits to the state by conserving and managing game species. Activities include collection 

[9:2:58,385] and assessment of information on the distribution and abundance of game fish and wildlife to determine appropriate 

[9:3:58,394] regulations (bag limits, gear restrictions, etc.) and to monitor the effects of those regulations. 

[10:0:58,412] 2600 - MANAGEMENT OF DEPARTMENT LANDS AND FACILITIES PROGRAM 

[11:0:58,430] This program manages Department-owned or leased lands and facilities, including hatcheries, wildlife areas, ecological 

[11:1:58,439] reserves, fish and wildlife laboratories, and public access areas, to contribute to the conservation, protection, and 

[11:2:58,448] management of fish and wildlife. 

[12:0:58,466] 2605 - ENFORCEMENT 

[13:0:58,484] This program serves the public through law enforcement, public safety and hunter education. Law enforcement promotes 

[13:1:58,493] compliance with laws and regulations protecting fish and wildlife resources; investigates habitat destruction, pollution 

[13:2:58,502] incidents and illegal commercialization of wildlife. Wardens also serve the public through general law enforcement, mutual 

[13:3:58,511] aid and homeland security. 

[14:0:58,529] 2610 - COMMUNICATIONS, EDUCATION, AND OUTREACH PROGRAM 

[15:0:58,547] This program serves the public through resource conservation education and use activities in the classroom and on public 

[15:1:58,556] and private lands, community and stakeholder outreach, and the delivery of information and data using a variety of methods 

[15:2:58,565] including publications, presentations, web applications and media relations. 

[16:0:58,583] 2615 - SPILL PREVENTION AND RESPONSE PROGRAM 

[17:0:58,601] This program prevents damage, minimizes environmental impacts, restores, and rehabilitates California's fish and wildlife 

[17:1:58,610] populations and their habitats from the harmful effects of oil and other deleterious material spills in marine waters and inland 

[17:2:58,619] habitats. 

[18:0:58,637] 2620 - FISH AND GAME COMMISSION 

[19:0:58,655] The California Fish and Game Commission ensures the long term sustainability of California's fish and wildlife resources by 

[19:1:58,664] guiding the ongoing scientific evaluation and assessment of California's fish and wildlife resources; setting California's fish 

[19:2:58,673] and wildlife resource management policies and ensuring these are implemented by the Department of Fish and Wildlife; 

[19:3:58,682] establishing appropriate fish and wildlife resource management rules and regulations; and building active fish and wildlife 

[19:4:58,691] resource management partnerships with individual landowners, the public and interest groups, and federal, state and local 

[19:5:58,700] resource management agencies. 

[20:0:58,745] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:58,89] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW 

[3:0:58,108] The Department of Fish and Wildlife manages 742 properties statewide, comprising more than 1 million acres 

[3:1:58,117] (approximately 679,000 acres owned by the state and 484,000 acres owned by other entities, but managed by the 

[3:2:58,126] Department. Several state agencies purchase land for the purpose of habitat or wildlife protection, and management 

[3:3:58,135] responsibilities for these properties are often transferred to the Department; therefore, the amount of land under Department 

[3:4:58,144] control continues to increase. The properties managed by the Department include: 111 wildlife areas, 136 ecological 

[3:5:58,153] reserves, 138 public access areas, and 20 fish hatcheries.

[13:0:197,606] 3720 California Coastal Commission 

[13:1:58,627] The California Coastal Commission, comprised of 12 voting members appointed equally by the Governor, the Senate Rules 

[13:2:58,636] Committee, and the Speaker of the Assembly, was created by voter initiative in 1972 and was made permanent by the 

[13:3:58,645] California Coastal Act of 1976 (Coastal Act). The Coastal Act calls for the protection and enhancement of public access and 

[13:4:58,654] recreation, marine resources, environmentally sensitive habitat areas, marine water quality, agriculture, and scenic 

[14:0:58,663] resources, and makes provisions for coastal-dependent industrial and energy development. New development in the coastal 

[14:1:58,672] zone requires a coastal permit either from local government or the Commission. Local governments are required to prepare 

[14:2:58,681] a local coastal program (LCP) for the coastal zone portion of their jurisdiction. After an LCP has been reviewed and 

[14:3:58,690] approved by the Commission as being consistent with the Coastal Act, the Commission's regulatory authority over most 

[14:4:58,699] types of new development is delegated to the local government, subject to limited appeals to the Commission. The 

[15:0:58,708] Commission also is designated the principal state coastal management agency for the purpose of administering the federal 

[15:1:58,717] Coastal Zone Management Act in California and has exclusive regulatory authority over federal activities such as permits, 

[15:2:58,726] leases, federal development projects, and other federal actions that could affect coastal zone resources and that would not 

[16:0:58,745] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[6:0:58,177] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:0:58,204] 2730 - COASTAL MANAGEMENT PROGRAM 

[8:0:58,222] The objectives of the Coastal Management Program are to implement coastal resources conservation through planning and 

[8:1:58,231] regulation. Activities include: 

[9:0:58,238] • 

[9:1:58,256] • 

[10:0:68,240] Reviewing and approval of local coastal programs (LCPs), port master plans, university long-range development plans, 

[10:1:68,249] and any amendments to such plans, for consistency with the Coastal Act. 

[11:0:68,258] Reviewing coastal development permit applications for new development in areas without a certified LCP, areas of 

[11:1:68,267] permanently retained jurisdiction (e.g., tidelands, submerged lands, and public trust lands) and limited categories of local 

[11:2:68,276] coastal development permit actions that can be appealed to the Commission. 

[11:3:68,285] Monitoring and enforcement of coastal development permits. 

[11:4:68,294] Reviewing federal activities for consistency with the Coastal Act. 

[11:5:68,303] Protecting and expanding opportunities for public coastal access and recreation. 

[11:6:68,312] Implementing a coastal water quality protection program. 

[12:0:58,283] • 

[12:1:58,292] • 

[12:2:58,301] • 

[12:3:58,310] • 

[12:4:58,319] • 

[12:5:58,328] • 

[13:0:68,321] Providing technical information and assistance to support effective coastal management. 

[13:1:68,330] Implementing a coastal and ocean resource public education program. 

[13:2:58,348] 2735 - COASTAL ENERGY PROGRAM 

[14:0:58,366] The Coastal Energy Program addresses coastal energy issues including, but not limited to, offshore oil and gas 

[14:1:58,375] development, electricity generating power plant expansion and development, and siting and development of liquefied natural 

[14:2:58,384] gas facilities. 

[14:3:58,402] 9900100 - ADMINISTRATION 

[15:0:58,420] The objective of the Administration Program is to provide administrative support including accounting, budgeting, business 

[15:1:58,429] services, support services, information technology, and human resources services to other departmental programs.

[9:0:208,475] 3760 State Coastal Conservancy 

[9:1:58,495] The State Coastal Conservancy develops and implements projects to protect, restore and enhance natural, recreational, and 

[9:2:58,504] economic resources along California's coast, coastal watersheds, the ocean, and within the San Francisco Bay Area. The 

[9:3:58,513] Coastal Conservancy supports implementation of the San Francisco Bay Area Conservancy Program and the Santa Ana 

[9:4:58,522] River Conservancy Program. Through technical assistance, direct funding and grants to local agencies and nonprofit 

[9:5:58,531] organizations, the Coastal Conservancy supports projects that: 

[10:0:58,547] • 

[10:1:58,565] • 

[10:2:58,574] • 

[10:3:58,583] • 

[10:4:58,592] • 

[10:5:58,601] • 

[11:0:68,549] Protect and enhance the natural environment, agricultural lands, scenic lands and urban waterfronts along the Coast and 

[11:1:68,558] around San Francisco Bay.  

[11:2:68,567] Increase public access to the coast and San Francisco Bay 

[11:3:68,576] Build trails and increase outdoor recreational opportunities.  

[11:4:68,585] Enhance coastal wetlands and other important habitat areas. 

[12:0:68,594] Prepare for and reduce the unavoidable impacts of climate change on the coastal region and San Francisco Bay Area. 

[12:1:68,603] Support education programs about coastal resources for kindergarten through grade 12.

[8:0:58,373] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[9:0:58,401] 2790 - COASTAL CONSERVANCY PROGRAM 

[9:1:58,419] The Coastal Conservancy seeks to achieve the following goals: 

[10:0:58,425] • 

[10:1:58,443] • 

[10:2:58,461] • 

[10:3:58,470] • 

[11:0:68,428] Protect and enhance habitats, wetlands, scenic open space, working lands and urban waterfronts along the California 

[11:1:68,437] coast and around San Francisco Bay.  

[12:0:68,446] Increase public access to the coast and outdoor recreational opportunities by creating new parks, trails and recreational 

[12:1:68,455] amenities.  

[13:0:68,464] Prepare for and reduce the unavoidable impacts of climate change on the coastal region and San Francisco Bay Area. 

[13:1:68,473] Support education programs about coastal resources for kindergarten through grade 12.  

[13:2:58,491] 2805 - STATE COASTAL CONSERVANCY LOCAL ASSISTANCE PROGRAM 

[14:0:58,509] The State Coastal Conservancy provides grants and technical assistance to local governments, nonprofits and other project 

[14:1:58,518] partners for the acquisition of land and easements, the development of public facilities, and the restoration of habitat. The 

[14:2:58,527] State Coastal Conservancy helps to implement the State's federally approved Coastal Management Program. The 

[14:3:58,536] Conservancy has participated in the protection of more than 390,000 acres of coastal lands through acquisition of fee title 

[14:4:58,545] and conservation easements. The Conservancy has helped restore more than 33,000 acres of habitat. 

[14:5:58,563] Through its projects, the Conservancy has established approximately 160 new coastal accessways and built more than 168 

[14:6:58,572] miles of new trails. 

[15:0:58,590] State Coastal Conservancy funds are primarily expended for purposes of local assistance but may also be expended for 

[15:1:58,599] capital outlay purposes, see Program 2820. 

[15:2:58,617] 2820 - STATE COASTAL CONSERVANCY CAPITAL OUTLAY PROGRAM 

[16:0:58,635] The State Coastal Conservancy provides grants and technical assistance to state agencies for the acquisition of land and 

[16:1:58,644] easements, the development of public facilities, and the restoration of habitat. The State Coastal Conservancy helps to 

[16:2:58,653] implement the State's federally approved Coastal Management Program. The Conservancy has participated in the protection 

[16:3:58,662] of more than 390,000 acres of coastal lands through acquisition of fee title and conservation easements. The Conservancy 

[16:4:58,671] has helped restore more than 33,000 acres of habitat. 

[17:0:58,689] Through its projects, the Conservancy has established approximately 40 new coastal accessways and built more than 42 

[17:1:58,698] miles of new trails. 

[18:0:58,745] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[13:0:182,613] 3790 Department of Parks and Recreation 

[13:1:58,634] The mission of the California Department of Parks and Recreation (Parks) is to provide for the health, inspiration, and 

[13:2:58,643] education of the people of California by helping to preserve the state's extraordinary biological diversity, protecting its most 

[13:3:58,652] valued natural, cultural and historical resources, and creating opportunities for high-quality outdoor recreation for current and 

[13:4:58,661] future generations to enjoy. With increased urbanization, the establishment of park units and recreation areas accessible to 

[13:5:58,670] the major population centers of the state has become particularly important. Specific activities include: stewardship of natural 

[13:6:58,679] resources, historic, cultural and archeological sites, and artifacts and structures; provision of interpretive services for park 

[13:7:58,688] visitors; construction and maintenance of campsites, trails, visitor centers, museums, and infrastructure such as roads and 

[13:8:58,697] water systems; and creation of recreational opportunities such as hiking, bicycling, fishing, swimming, horseback riding, 

[13:9:58,706] jogging, camping, picnicking, and off-highway vehicle recreation. In addition, the Division of Boating and Waterways funds, 

[13:10:58,715] plans, and develops boating facilities on waterways throughout California and ensures safe boating for the public by 

[13:11:58,724] providing financial aid and training to local law enforcement agencies. 

[14:0:58,745] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[6:0:58,285] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW 

[7:0:58,303] The system consists of 280 parks, beaches, trails, wildlife areas, open spaces, off-highway vehicle areas, and historic sites. 

[7:1:58,312] Parks is responsible for approximately 1.6 million acres of land, including over 343 miles of coastline, 984 miles of lake, 

[7:2:58,321] reservoir and river frontage, approximately 15,000 campsites and alternative camping facilities, and 5,124 miles of non-

[7:3:58,330] motorized trails. 

[8:0:58,348] Over the past five years, Parks has expended approximately $130 million to develop and expand the state park system. 

[8:1:58,357] Parks has accepted gifts and other donations of property and historic structures at no cost to the state when those donations 

[8:2:58,366] made programmatic sense.

[1:0:176,44] 3810 Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy 

[2:0:58,64] The Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy acquires, restores, and consolidates lands in the Santa Monica Mountains, Rim 

[2:1:58,73] of the Valley Trail Corridor, Upper Los Angeles River Watershed, and watersheds of Santa Monica Bay for park, recreation, 

[2:2:58,82] and conservation purposes. The Conservancy may: 

[2:3:68,100] Lease, rent, sell, transfer, or exchange lands for park purposes. 

[2:4:68,109] Award grants or interest free loans to state and local agencies to purchase or restore park, recreation, conservation, or 

[2:5:68,118] buffer-zone areas to ensure that the character and intensity of development on these lands is generally compatible with 

[2:6:68,127] and does not adversely impact the environment. 

[3:0:58,98] • 

[3:1:58,107] • 

[4:0:58,134] • 

[4:1:58,152] • 

[4:2:58,161] • 

[5:0:68,136] Implement programs to improve access from the inner city areas surrounding the Conservancy, thereby providing 

[5:1:68,145] recreational opportunities for all residents. 

[5:2:68,154] Manage the Rim of the Valley Trail Corridor recreational trail. 

[5:3:68,163] Plans for restoration, recreation, and conservation projects for the Los Angeles River and Upper Los Angeles River 

[5:4:68,172] Watershed.

[0:0:58,15] 2017-18 STATE BUDGET — RES 190   NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:102,44] 3820 San Francisco Bay Conservation and Development Commission 

[1:1:58,64] The San Francisco Bay Conservation and Development Commission is a state planning and regulatory agency with regional 

[1:2:58,73] authority. Its mission is to protect and enhance San Francisco Bay and to encourage the Bay's responsible and productive 

[1:3:58,82] use for this and future generations. The Commission authored and maintains the San Francisco Bay Plan and relies on it, 

[1:4:58,91] the McAteer-Petris Act, and other regulatory authority to maximize public access to the Bay and minimize Bay fill. The 

[1:5:58,100] Commission issues permits for filling, dredging, and development projects within the Bay, along the Bay shoreline, and 

[1:6:58,109] within salt ponds and certain managed wetlands adjacent to the Bay. The Commission also implements the Suisun Marsh 

[1:7:58,118] Preservation Act of 1977. By state statute, the Commission develops and implements the federal Coastal Zone Management 

[1:8:58,127] Act's program for the Bay and exercises authority over federal activities otherwise not subject to state control. The 

[1:9:58,136] Commission leads the ongoing multi-agency regional effort to address the impacts of sea level rise and climate change on 

[1:10:58,145] the Bay and its environs. Funding for these efforts to address climate change is derived mainly from federal grants and other 

[1:11:58,154] agreements, contracts, and reimbursements. 

[2:0:58,181] 3-YR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS

[11:0:202,577] 3840 Delta Protection Commission 

[11:1:58,597] The mission of the Delta Protection Commission is to protect, maintain, enhance and enrich the overall quality of the Delta 

[11:2:58,606] environment and economy, with a focus on agriculture, recreation and natural resources, and mindful of the importance of 

[11:3:58,615] the Delta to all Californians. The Commission ensures orderly, balanced conservation and development of Delta land 

[11:4:58,624] resources and provides a forum for Delta residents to engage in decisions regarding actions to recognize and enhance the 

[11:5:58,633] unique cultural, recreational, and agricultural resources of the Delta. The Commission identifies and makes 

[12:0:58,642] recommendations on methods of preserving the Delta as an evolving place, and promotes Delta legacy communities, 

[12:1:58,651] regional economic sustainability, and emergency response preparedness.
